<p><b>Sotho</b> (<span class="rt-commentedText nowrap"><span class="IPA nopopups noexcerpt" lang="en-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/English" title="Help:IPA/English">/<span style="border-bottom:1px dotted"><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/ɛ/: &#39;e&#39; in &#39;dress&#39;">ɛ</span><span title="/ˈ/: primary stress follows">ˈ</span><span title="&#39;s&#39; in &#39;sigh&#39;">s</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span><span title="&#39;t&#39; in &#39;tie&#39;">t</span><span title="/uː/: &#39;oo&#39; in &#39;goose&#39;">uː</span></span>/</a></span></span>)<sup id="cite_ref-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-4">&#91;a&#93;</a></sup> <i>Sesotho</i>, also known as <b>Southern Sotho</b> or <i>Sesotho sa Borwa</i> is a <a href="/wiki/Southern_Bantu_languages" title="Southern Bantu languages">Southern Bantu language</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Sotho%E2%80%93Tswana_languages" title="Sotho–Tswana languages">Sotho–Tswana</a> ("S.30") group, spoken in <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, and <a href="/wiki/South_Africa" title="South Africa">South Africa</a> where it is an <a href="/wiki/Official_language" title="Official language">official language</a>;
</p><p>Like all <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu languages</a>, Sesotho is an <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative language</a> that uses numerous <a href="/wiki/Affixes" class="mw-redirect" title="Affixes">affixes</a> and derivational and inflexional rules to build <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_grammar#The_Sesotho_word" title="Sesotho grammar">complete words</a>.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Classification">Classification</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Classification"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sotho is a <a href="/wiki/Southern_Bantu_languages" title="Southern Bantu languages">Southern Bantu language</a> belonging to the <a href="/wiki/Niger%E2%80%93Congo_languages" title="Niger–Congo languages">Niger–Congo language family</a> within the <a href="/wiki/Sotho-Tswana_languages" class="mw-redirect" title="Sotho-Tswana languages">Sotho-Tswana</a> branch of <a href="/wiki/Guthrie_classification_of_Bantu_languages#Zone_S" title="Guthrie classification of Bantu languages">Zone S (S.30)</a>.
</p><p>"Sotho" is also the name given to the entire Sotho-Tswana group, in which case Sesotho proper is called "Southern Sotho". Within the Sotho-Tswana group, Southern Sotho is also related to <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">Lozi</a> (<i>Silozi</i>), with which it forms the Sesotho-Lozi group within Sotho-Tswana.
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sotho" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Sotho">Northern Sotho</a> group is geographical, and includes a number of dialects also closely related to Sotho-Lozi. <a href="/wiki/Tswana_language" title="Tswana language">Tswana</a> is also known as "Western Sesotho".
</p><p>The Sotho-Tswana group is in turn closely related to the other <a href="/wiki/Southern_Bantu" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern Bantu">Southern Bantu</a> languages, including the <a href="/wiki/Venda_language" title="Venda language">Venda</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tsonga_language" title="Tsonga language">Tsonga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Tonga_language_(Mozambique)" title="Tonga language (Mozambique)">Tonga</a>, <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">Lozi</a> which is native to <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambia</a> and the other surrounding <a href="/wiki/Southern_African" class="mw-redirect" title="Southern African">Southern African countries</a> and <a href="/wiki/Nguni_languages" title="Nguni languages">Nguni languages</a>, and possibly<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (September 2015)">clarification needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> also the <a href="/wiki/Makua_languages" title="Makua languages">Makua</a> (zone P) languages of <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Mozambique" title="Mozambique">Mozambique</a>.
</p><p><i>Sotho</i> is the root word. Various prefixes may be added for specific derivations, such as <i>Sesotho</i> for the Sotho language and <i>Basotho</i> for the <a href="/wiki/Sotho_people" title="Sotho people">Sotho people</a>. Use of <i>Sesotho</i> rather than <i>Sotho</i> for the language in English has seen increasing use since the 1980s, especially in <a href="/wiki/South_African_English" title="South African English">South African English</a> and in Lesotho.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Dialects">Dialects</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=2" title="Edit section: Dialects"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-left" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg/220px-National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg" decoding="async" width="220" height="306" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/6a/National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg/330px-National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/6/6a/National_Women%27s_Day_detail_Sesotho.jpg 2x" data-file-width="368" data-file-height="512" /></a><figcaption>A Mosotho woman holding up a sign protesting violence against women, written in her native Sesotho language, at a <a href="/wiki/National_Women%27s_Day" title="National Women&#39;s Day">National Women's Day</a> protest at the National University of Lesotho. The sign translates: "If you do not listen to women, we will lose patience with you." (2008)</figcaption></figure>
<p>Except for faint lexical variation within Lesotho, and for marked lexical variation between the Lesotho/<a href="/wiki/Free_State_(province)" title="Free State (province)">Free State</a> variety and that of the large urban townships to the north (such as <a href="/wiki/Soweto" title="Soweto">Soweto</a>) due to heavy borrowing from neighbouring languages, there is no discernible dialect variation in this language.
</p><p>However, one point that seems to often confuse authors who attempt to study the dialectology of Sesotho is the term <i><a href="/wiki/Basotho" class="mw-redirect" title="Basotho">Basotho</a></i>, which can variously mean "<a href="/wiki/Sotho-Tswana_peoples" title="Sotho-Tswana peoples">Sotho–Tswana speakers</a>", "Southern Sotho and <a href="/wiki/Northern_Sotho_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Northern Sotho people">Northern Sotho speakers</a>", "Sesotho speakers", and "residents of Lesotho." The <a href="/wiki/Nguni_languages" title="Nguni languages">Nguni language</a> <a href="/wiki/Phuthi_language" title="Phuthi language">Phuthi</a> has been heavily influenced by Sesotho; its speakers have mixed Nguni and Sotho–Tswana ancestry. It seems that it is sometimes treated erroneously as a dialect of Sesotho called "Sephuthi." However, Phuthi is mutually unintelligible with standard Sesotho and thus cannot in any sense be termed a dialect of it. The occasional tendency to label all minor languages spoken in Lesotho as "dialects" of Sesotho is considered patronising,<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Manual_of_Style/Words_to_watch#Unsupported_attributions" title="Wikipedia:Manual of Style/Words to watch"><span title="The material near this tag may use weasel words or too-vague attribution. (July 2019)">by whom?</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> in addition to being linguistically inaccurate and in part serving a national myth that all citizens of Lesotho have Sesotho as their mother tongue.
</p><p>Additionally, being derived from a language or dialect very closely related to modern Sesotho,<sup id="cite_ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5">&#91;b&#93;</a></sup> the <a href="/wiki/Zambia" title="Zambia">Zambian</a> Sotho–Tswana language <a href="/wiki/Lozi_language" title="Lozi language">Lozi</a> is also sometimes cited as a modern dialect of Sesotho named <i>Serotse</i> or <i>Sekololo</i>.
</p><p>The oral history of the Basotho and Northern Sotho peoples (as contained in their <a href="/wiki/Diboko" title="Diboko">liboko</a>) states that 'Mathulare, a daughter of the chief of the <i>Bafokeng</i> nation (an old and respected people), was married to chief Tabane of the (Southern) <i>Bakgatla</i> (a branch of the <i>Bahurutse</i>, who are one of the most ancient of the Sotho–Tswana tribes), and bore the founders of five tribes: <i>Bapedi</i> (by Mopedi), <i>Makgolokwe</i> (by Kgetsi), <i>Baphuthing</i> (by Mophuthing, and later the Mzizi of <a href="/wiki/Dlamini" title="Dlamini">Dlamini</a>, connected with the present-day <a href="/wiki/Ndebele_people_(Zimbabwe)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ndebele people (Zimbabwe)">Ndebele</a>), <i>Batlokwa</i> (by Kgwadi), and <i>Basia</i> (by Mosia). These were the first peoples to be called "Basotho", before many of their descendants and other peoples came together to form <a href="/wiki/Moshoeshoe_I" title="Moshoeshoe I">Moshoeshoe I</a>'s nation in the early 19th century. The situation is even further complicated by various historical factors, such as members of parent clans joining their descendants or various clans calling themselves by the same names (because they honour the same legendary ancestor or have the same totem).
</p><p>An often repeated story is that when the modern Basotho nation was established by King <a href="/wiki/Moshoeshoe_I" title="Moshoeshoe I">Moshoeshoe I</a>, his own "dialect" Sekwena was chosen over two other popular variations Setlokwa and Setaung and that these two still exist as "dialects" of modern Sesotho.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (February 2016)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup> The inclusion of Setlokwa in this scenario is confusing, as the modern language named "Setlokwa" is a Northern Sesotho language spoken by descendants of the same Batlokwa whose attack on the young chief Moshoeshoe's settlement during <a href="/wiki/Difaqane" class="mw-redirect" title="Difaqane">Lifaqane</a> (led by the famous widow <a href="/wiki/Mmanthatisi" title="Mmanthatisi">Mmanthatisi</a>) caused them to migrate to present-day Lesotho. On the other hand, Doke &amp; Mofokeng claims that the tendency of many Sesotho speakers to say for example <i>ke ronngwe</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kʼɪʀʊŋ̩ŋʷe]</span> instead of <i>ke romilwe</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[kʼɪʀuˌmilʷe]</span> when forming the perfect of the passive of verbs ending in <i>-ma</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mɑ]</span> (as well as forming their perfects with <i>-mme</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[m̩me]</span> instead of <i>-mile</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[mile]</span>) is "a relic of the extinct Tlokwa dialect".
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Geographic_distribution">Geographic distribution</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Geographic distribution"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>

color:white;">&#160;</span>&#160;&gt;3000 /km²</div></li></ul></div></figcaption></figure>
<p>According to the <a href="/wiki/South_African_National_Census_of_2011" class="mw-redirect" title="South African National Census of 2011">South African National Census of 2011</a>, there were almost four million first language Sesotho speakers recorded in South Africa – approximately eight per cent of the population. Most Sesotho speakers in South Africa reside in <a href="/wiki/Free_State_(province)" title="Free State (province)">Free State</a> and <a href="/wiki/Gauteng" title="Gauteng">Gauteng</a>. Sesotho is also the main language spoken by the people of <a href="/wiki/Lesotho" title="Lesotho">Lesotho</a>, where, according to 1993 data, it was spoken by about 1,493,000 people, or 85% of the population. The census fails to record other South Africans for whom Sesotho is a second or third language. Such speakers are found in all major residential areas of <a href="/wiki/Metropolitan_municipality_(South_Africa)" title="Metropolitan municipality (South Africa)">Metropolitan Municipalities</a> – such as <a href="/wiki/Johannesburg" title="Johannesburg">Johannesburg</a>, and the <a href="/wiki/Vaal_Triangle" title="Vaal Triangle">Vaal Triangle</a> – where multilingualism and polylectalism are very high.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">&#91;<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (June 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>&#93;</sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Official_status">Official status</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Official status"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<p>Sesotho is one of the twelve <a href="/wiki/Official_languages_of_South_Africa" class="mw-redirect" title="Official languages of South Africa">official languages of South Africa</a>, one of the two official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Lesotho" title="Languages of Lesotho">languages of Lesotho</a> and one of the sixteen official <a href="/wiki/Languages_of_Zimbabwe" title="Languages of Zimbabwe">languages of Zimbabwe</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Derived_languages">Derived languages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Derived languages"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<p>Sesotho is one of the many languages from which <a href="/wiki/Tsotsitaal" class="mw-redirect" title="Tsotsitaal">tsotsitaals</a> are derived. Tsotsitaal is not a proper language, as it is primarily a unique vocabulary and a set of idioms but used with the grammar and inflexion rules of another language (usually Sesotho or <a href="/wiki/Zulu_language" title="Zulu language">Zulu</a>). It is a part of the youth culture in most Southern <a href="/wiki/Gauteng" title="Gauteng">Gauteng</a> <a href="/wiki/Township_(South_Africa)" title="Township (South Africa)">townships</a> and is the primary language used in <a href="/wiki/Kwaito" title="Kwaito">Kwaito music</a>.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Phonology">Phonology</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: Phonology"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_phonology" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesotho phonology">Sesotho phonology</a></div>
<p>The sound system of Sesotho is unusual in many respects. It has <a href="/wiki/Ejective_consonant" title="Ejective consonant">ejective consonants</a>, <a href="/wiki/Click_consonant" title="Click consonant">click consonants</a>, a <a href="/wiki/Uvular_trill" class="mw-redirect" title="Uvular trill">uvular trill</a>, a relatively large number of <a href="/wiki/Affricate_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Affricate consonant">affricate consonants</a>, no <a href="/wiki/Prenasalised_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Prenasalised consonant">prenasalised consonants</a>, and a rare form of vowel-height (alternatively, <a href="/wiki/Advanced_tongue_root" class="mw-redirect" title="Advanced tongue root">advanced tongue root</a>) harmony. In total, the language contains some 39 consonantal<sup id="cite_ref-6" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-6">&#91;c&#93;</a></sup> and 9 vowel <a href="/wiki/Phonemes" class="mw-redirect" title="Phonemes">phonemes</a>.
</p><p>It also has a large number of complex sound transformations which often change the phones of words due to the influence of other (sometimes invisible) sounds.
</p>

<td></td>

<th colspan="2"><a href="/wiki/Trill_consonant" title="Trill consonant">Trill</a>
</th>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td></td>
<td><span class="IPA" lang="und-fonipa"><a href="/wiki/Voiced_uvular_trill" title="Voiced uvular trill">ʀ</a></span></td>
<td>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<ol><li><span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[d]</span> is an <a href="/wiki/Allophone" title="Allophone">allophone</a> of <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span>, occurring only before the close vowels (<span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/i/</span> and <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/u/</span>). Dialectical evidence shows that in the Sotho–Tswana languages <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">/l/</span> was originally pronounced as a <a href="/wiki/Retroflex_flap" class="mw-redirect" title="Retroflex flap">retroflex flap</a> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[ɽ]</span> before the two close vowels.</li></ol>
<p>Sesotho makes a three-way distinction between lightly <a href="/wiki/Ejective_consonant" title="Ejective consonant">ejective</a>, <a href="/wiki/Aspiration_(phonetics)" class="mw-redirect" title="Aspiration (phonetics)">aspirated</a> and <a href="/wiki/Voiced_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Voiced consonant">voiced</a> <a href="/wiki/Stop_consonant" class="mw-redirect" title="Stop consonant">stops</a> in several <a href="/wiki/Place_of_articulation" title="Place of articulation">places of articulation</a>.
</p><p>The standard Sesotho clicks tend to be substituted with dental clicks in regular speech.
</p>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Orthography">Orthography</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Orthography"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_orthography" title="Sesotho orthography">Sesotho orthography</a></div>
<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Grammar">Grammar</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Grammar"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096"><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_grammar" title="Sesotho grammar">Sesotho grammar</a></div>
<p>The most striking properties of Sesotho grammar, and the most important properties which reveal it as a <a href="/wiki/Bantu_languages" title="Bantu languages">Bantu</a> language, are its <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_nouns" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesotho nouns">noun gender</a> and <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_concords" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesotho concords">concord</a> systems. The grammatical gender system does not encode sex gender, and indeed, Bantu languages in general are not <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_marker" class="mw-redirect" title="Grammatical marker">grammatically marked</a> for gender.
</p><p>Another well-known property of the Bantu languages is their <a href="/wiki/Agglutinative_language" title="Agglutinative language">agglutinative</a> morphology. Additionally, they tend to lack any <a href="/wiki/Grammatical_case" title="Grammatical case">grammatical case</a> systems, indicating noun roles almost exclusively through word order.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Notes">Notes</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Sotho_language&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Notes"><span>edit</span></a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1011085734">.mw-parser-output .reflist{font-size:90%;margin-bottom:0.5em;list-style-type:decimal}.mw-parser-output .reflist .references{font-size:100%;margin-bottom:0;list-style-type:inherit}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-2{column-width:30em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns-3{column-width:25em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns{margin-top:0.3em}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns ol{margin-top:0}.mw-parser-output .reflist-columns li{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-alpha{list-style-type:upper-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-upper-roman{list-style-type:upper-roman}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-alpha{list-style-type:lower-alpha}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-greek{list-style-type:lower-greek}.mw-parser-output .reflist-lower-roman{list-style-type:lower-roman}</style><div class="reflist reflist-lower-alpha">
<div class="mw-references-wrap"><ol class="references">
<li id="cite_note-4"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-4">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">Also spelled <i>Suthio</i>, or <i>Suthu</i>, <i>Souto</i>, <i>Sisutho</i>, <i>Sutu</i>, or <i>Sesutu</i>.</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-5"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-5">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">To the extent that it even has several words that resemble Sesotho words with clicks:
<dl><dd><i>ku kala</i> to begin (Sesotho <i>ho qala</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[hʊǃɑlɑ]</span>)</dd>
<dd><i>ku kabana</i> to quarrel (Sesotho <i>ho qabana</i> <span class="IPA nowrap" lang="und-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)">[hʊǃɑbɑnɑ]</span>),</dd></dl>
one could just as easily say that these words were imported from Nguni languages (<i>ukuqala</i> and <i>ukuxabana</i>, which is where the Sesotho versions come from), and the language does also contain words resembling click words from Nguni but not from Sesotho (such as <i>ku kabanga</i> to think, c.f. Zulu <i>ukucabanga</i>).</span>
</li>
<li id="cite_note-6"><span class="mw-cite-backlink"><b><a href="#cite_ref-6">^</a></b></span> <span class="reference-text">75 if you include the <a href="/wiki/Sesotho_phonology#pp_labialization" class="mw-redirect" title="Sesotho phonology">labialized</a> consonants.</span>
</li>
</ol></div></div>

<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><span><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/40px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/60px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/dd/Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg/80px-Wikivoyage-Logo-v3-icon.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="193" data-file-height="193" /></span></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikivoyage has a phrasebook for <i><b><a href="https://en.wikivoyage.org/wiki/Sesotho_phrasebook#Q34340" class="extiw" title="wikivoyage:Sesotho phrasebook">Sesotho</a></b></i>.</div></div>

<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://sesotho.blogspot.com">Weblog on Sesotho</a></li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://translate.org.za/">Translate.org.za</a> Project translating Free and Open Source Software into South African languages, including Sesotho.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://www.panafril10n.org/wikidoc/pmwiki.php/PanAfrLoc/Sesotho">PanAfrican L10n page on Sesotho</a> Information on the language and localisation.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="http://friendsoflesotho.org/files/puo_ea_sechaba.pdf">Sesotho language tutorial</a> A beginner's language text, created for the US Peace Corps.</li>
<li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://www.scribd.com/doc/71077505/Sesotho-Language-Manual">the SeSotho book</a>  Another book developed by a Peace Corps volunteer</li></ul>

<ul><li><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070114015602/http://translate.org.za/content/view/1610/54/">Spell checker for OpenOffice.org and Mozilla</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070324112647/http://translate.org.za/content/view/17/32/">OpenOffice.org</a>, <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070210160756/http://translate.org.za/content/view/1611/54/">Mozilla Firefox web-browser</a>, and <a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://web.archive.org/web/20070324203655/http://translate.org.za/content/view/1612/54/">Mozilla Thunderbird email program</a> in Sesotho.</li></ul>
